I confess I have never read all the Land standing orders. When I was in command I never even read the district standing orders. No one does. The noble Viscount is smiling at me because he knows that I am right. The reality is that no one reads all the reports or all the orders. If you want them all read and complied with, or at least for people to try to comply with them all, you would need to employ a captain at every single unit to be a compliance officer.

In 1998, as officer commanding a TA company, one document I studied very carefully, because if the worst happened I would not have the opportunity to study it in time, was the casualty procedure—a point referred to by the noble Viscount. But I am not completely confident with Fiona Murphy's analysis of that procedure in Annex C. I am not fully convinced that she researched her conclusions. For instance, she queries why the casualty procedure is a classified document marked "Restricted". If she had asked any officer, he would have explained that "Restricted" is the lowest security classification possible. The workshop manual for a Land Rover is graded "Restricted". "Restricted" really means "don't give the whole document to the media". It seems to me that she did not arrange for a sanity check by an
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column 1119
 
experienced retired officer, but I also think that she does not understand the challenges of casualty notification. Any casualty notification system needs to be able to deal with at least 1,000 casualties in one day, but, at the same time, the next of kin needs to be notified within about two hours of the casualty occurring. In addition, the notifying officers need to be course-trained. You cannot use any officer; they must know what they are doing because it is such a sensitive task.

Bad news travels very fast today, but I was surprised when, a few months ago, it appeared that a next of kin was notified of a casualty at 3 am. That was completely contrary to the casualty report system that I read in 1998, but the procedure has been updated. This had been made necessary by modern communications equipment, particularly the mobile phone and the internet. I know some casualty notification officers very well indeed. My information is that the casualty procedure system is fit for purpose, and that the Minister has no problems there.

However, Fiona Murphy's work uncovers an inexplicable and glaring failure. That is the apparent total lack of interest on the part of the commanding officers and other officers at Deepcut in the feelings of the families. It is no surprise that they began to suspect that something sinister may have happened. I certainly do not understand why each family was not invited to Deepcut at an early opportunity. I am sure that if the families had seen and sensed how upset the whole garrison would have been after each event, they might not so easily have become convinced that something had gone very wrong. I wish that Mr Blake had perhaps used his skill and looked a bit closer at why those visits did not occur. Perhaps somebody made a decision that the families were not to be invited to the garrison; perhaps orders were given that that could not happen.

Clearly, as we have all read in the report, something has gone very wrong. The report referred to sub-standard warrant officers who had been reduced to the rank. It stated that the Deepcut training regiment was, and perhaps still is, under-resourced in accommodation and manpower. We read about the lack of secure accommodation for female soldiers. Worst of all, we read about some—though they were only a minority—exceptionally poor-quality junior officers. I have never heard of an officer receiving a confidential report that was so damning. I think that the brigadier wrote, "I have absolutely no confidence in this officer at all". If I received a confidential report like that, I would say, "Well, boss, when do you want me to go?"

So in making these observations, I am not looking to allocate blame, but to express concern about how the Armed Forces are becoming progressively more vulnerable to events such as those at Deepcut. The Blake review explains in some detail how the Army put in hand a study, by Lieutenant Colonel Haes, to examine the supervisory ratios at training establishments. The pressures of contractorisation, giving priority to the frontline and general undermanning led to the reductions in the availability of sufficient good supervisors at training establishments.

The Haes report stated:

    "The dilemma is that military staff are being reduced at a time when the Duty of Care and Supervision needs of trainees appear to be increasing; a credibility gap is opening up".

Blake stated in his report that this was flatly rejected by the Army and he quoted its response:

    "In the current climate of undermanning and operational overstretch, it is self-evident that there is no possibility of achieving enhancements to the manning liability without compensating reductions and gapping of posts elsewhere. In short the study"—

Blake was referring to the Haes report—

    "has usefully highlighted a number of areas concerned but has not provided a practical solution".

I shall not rehearse the other reviews, internal or external, which have tried to push the MoD into spending adequate amounts of money on the personnel area. Accommodation standards are a continuing disgrace. The newly trumpeted PFI project sets a target of 2016 to complete improvements. We shall find that all those Blake recommendations, which have resource implications, will have to fight for their place in the defence programme against the demands of the equipment and operational areas.

Recommendation 26—the need for a military ombudsman—is the key recommendation of the Blake review, which the noble Lord, Lord Ashley of Stoke, mentioned. In my response to the Statement, I highlighted this recommendation. My full, in-depth reading of the Blake review has confirmed my support for this important proposal. We should remember that this is not the first time that such a proposal has been made. Independent representation was proposed by Sir Michael Bett in his Independent Review of the Armed Forces' Manpower, Career and Remuneration Structures, published in 1995. That report, in much easier operational times than now, found that 66 per cent of service men and women were in favour of some form of independent representation outside the chain of command. I would be interested to know from the Minister whether there has been a subsequent internal survey in the past decade to determine whether that level of support for some form of independent ombudsman or system is still there or is greater than it was in 1995.

An independent voice in redress procedures has been raised in the context of the Armed Forces Bill. The noble Earl, Lord Attlee, has pointed out that we
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column 1122
 
will want to come back to those recommendations when we deal with the Bill. I was delighted to see that the discussion that the Armed Forces Bill Select Committee had on 30 March 2006 was on an amendment that would have established an independent Armed Forces federation.

There is a growing call for the military to enjoy some of the rights that other citizens take for granted. At every stage the MoD resists allowing the Armed Forces a right to express concern outside the chain of command. This is not acceptable at this stage. The United States and Australia are among the many nations which welcome such independent arrangements. Since the advent of the new budgetary systems to the way defence works, every commanding officer is put in the position of managing a small business. While trying to manage his budget, he acts, at the same time, as the voice of concern for subordinates, which becomes ever more difficult.

The Blake review has got it right when it states in Chapter 12.98 that,

    "this Review believes that the present proposals in the Armed Forces Bill do not go far enough to ensure independent supervision and review of the discipline and complaints system. There is a danger that an historic opportunity will be lost for the Armed Forces to obtain independent assistance to achieve the goals they have set themselves to ensure the welfare of trainees and soldiers is effectively addressed".

The following paragraph states:

    "It will be difficult for the Armed Forces to satisfy the public that they have nothing to hide in the running of their discipline and complaints system if there is a perception of unwillingness to accept meaningful independent oversight, which is increasingly seen as a necessary counterweight to the powers and prerogatives of military life".

I trust that the Minister is in a position to tell us more of what the Government intend to do in following the recommendations in the Blake review and, in particular, whether they intend to follow the example of other countries and seek to establish a military ombudsman with, at minimum, the functions described by Blake in the Armed Forces Bill. We shall certainly expect to discuss that when the Bill comes to the House and also the associated but separate question of whether wider representation is needed through some form of Armed Forces federation. It is essential that the balance of resource allocation which the Ministry of Defence makes is redressed now towards the people who serve their country in the Armed Forces.

As I said in my Statement on 29 March this year repeating the announcement made in another place on the publication of the Deepcut review, we are grateful to Nicholas Blake QC for conducting such a thorough
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column 1125
 
review into the circumstances surrounding the deaths of the four young soldiers at Deepcut, and I join noble Lords in expressing our deep condolences to the families of the soldiers. It is about making sure that we strike the right balance when putting resources in place to support our people in the Ministry of Defence. The Blake review clearly raises issues that focus on whether or not that is being done. When we are in a position to do so, we will of course publish our response to the Deepcut review in full.

19 Apr 2006 : Column 1126
 
happened. He went on to say that he believed that, after suffering so much, the families were entitled to such an inquiry. Mr Blake recognised that one important reason underlining the calls by the families for a public inquiry was that, in the cases of Sean Benton, Geoff Gray and Cheryl James, they had not seen the material relating to their child's death that had come to light since the original inquests. I turn now to some of Mr Blake's recommendations. We welcome his conclusion that young people with suitable qualities for a military career should continue to be able to enlist at 16. Half of those who enter initial Army training are under 18. They are vital to the Army, but it is also important to acknowledge what the Army offers them. Mr Blake states that for many young people, including those who otherwise may not have an opportunity to lead structured and fulfilling lives, the Armed Forces offer broad opportunities for the acquisition of new skills and career development that schools and colleges may not. However, as the noble Lord, Lord Garden, and others have said, we recognise the special obligations that that places on us with regard to those young people, and commanding officers are well aware of the specific welfare needs of recruits and trainees under the age of 18.

We recognise the vital role that parents play in support of their children, both before and after they have joined the Armed Forces. Our recruiting officers make every effort to establish and sustain links with the parents or guardians of those who have applied to join the Armed Forces, and commanding officers are required to write to parents or guardians on the arrival of recruits under 18, explaining how they can contact the unit if they have any concerns that they wish to raise and providing information on the training that their son or daughter will undertake. Parents' days are held during courses, and parents are encouraged to attend the final passing-out ceremony.

We recognise the importance of instructors at service training establishments and already have work in hand to develop a "train the trainer" package, which includes modules on the care and welfare of trainees.
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column 1127
 
The first courses began last November. A new staff leadership school is due to be opened at Pirbright in 2007, where all Army instructors will be trained. The noble Lord, Lord Astor of Hever, has asked specifically about the progress that we are making in putting in place actions in response to the recommendations. This gives some examples of actions that we have been taking, but we recognise that there are areas where it is urgent for us to respond to the 34 recommendations set out in the Blake review.

Mr Blake concludes that there is no evidence that the four young soldiers were bullied to death at Deepcut but finds that some personnel there—perhaps only a small minority—experienced some form of harassment, discrimination or oppressive behaviour. That is unacceptable. We must address it as a matter of urgency. My noble friend has rightly stated that he has brought up the issue many times with the Ministry of Defence. It is incumbent on us as Ministers to do everything that we can to root out harassment and bullying in our Armed Forces. It is not a problem unique to the Armed Forces in this country; none the less we must do everything in our power to eliminate it.

At every stage of the training it is made clear to trainees that the Armed Forces do not tolerate any form of bullying and harassment. All service personnel are informed of the means by which they can bring any allegations of such conduct to the attention of the appropriate authority to be properly investigated. Recruits are issued with a training covenant card, encouraging them to report bullying and harassment and clearly setting out how a recruit should behave and how they can expect to be treated. We are looking at how we can best ensure that our personnel have the confidence to raise a grievance and the confidence that it will be dealt with quickly, effectively and fairly.

We have proposed improvements to our complaints system under the Armed Forces Bill. The proposal to establish a commissioner of military complaints will rightly and properly be debated at that time. Clause 27 [Assembly Commission]:

Lord Livsey of Talgarth moved Amendment No. 28:

    Page 17, line 12, at end insert "not belonging to the same political group"

The noble Lord said: Clause 27 has involved in it a simple principle, which is that we do not want people belonging to the same political group. This amendment concerns the Assembly Commission and its constitution of members of different political persuasions, and provides a fair representation on the
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column 1129
 
Assembly Commission itself. It is a principle of representation which we regard as extremely important as it provides a proper balance.

What is more important in one way, as far as this debate is concerned, is Amendment No. 32. The reasons for the changes we wish to make to this amendment will become apparent in a minute. On page 18, at line 9, we wish to leave out subsections (2) to (9) and insert:

    "(2) The members of any committee established by the Assembly under section 28(1)—

    (a) shall be elected by the Assembly from among the Assembly members, and

    (b) shall, unless that committee exists solely to provide advice, be elected so as to secure that, as far as is practicable, the balance of the parties in the Assembly is reflected in the membership of the committee".

That is a principle that we here at Westminster adhere to pretty strongly in committees of both Houses and it reflects the results of voting in elections, so there is membership of committees that follows that pattern.

Proposed new subsection (3) in the amendment states:

    "(3) The committees established by the Assembly under section 28(1) shall be, as far as is practicable, chaired by members of political parties in proportion to the number of members of the Assembly belonging to each party".

The effect of this is to remove the d'Hondt process of allocation from establishing the chair of the political parties involved in the Assembly Commission. The d'Hondt method is unsatisfactory and was abandoned by West Germany in 1970. It was also abandoned in its use by the Scottish Parliament because it was thought that there was not a fair representation.

I want to quote a few things in this debate. It was the intention of the Presiding Officer of the Assembly—the noble Lord, Lord Elis-Thomas—to be here today, but unfortunately he had to go to a funeral of a relatively young man who he knew well in Bala. He is sad not to be able to make representations about this part of the Bill. He is fully in support of the content of the Bill, with one exception—Clause 29, with which he is in total disagreement. It is the only part of the Bill that he feels strongly about.

We are informed in the Explanatory Notes that the clause is intended to lay down a formula for use in:

    "The allocation of seats on committees between different political groups . . . according to a d'Hondt formula . . . which is . . . used . . . to determine the allocation of electoral region seats in the Assembly for each region".

It is also used in,

    "section 29 of the Northern Ireland Act 1998 to allocate committee chairs and deputy chairs".

There is no doubt that this clause has been included to give a built-in advantage to the majority party in the National Assembly. Well, we can argue about that. D'Hondt invariably gives a disproportionate advantage to the majority party in contrast to the Sainte-Lague divisor rule, for instance. I am not going to go into all the niceties of all these formulas and formulations, but by abandoning the d'Hondt principle a fairer result would occur if the Assembly itself made the decision on securing a proper balance.

On Second Reading, noble Lords expressed concern about Clause 29 and its impact on the working of the National Assembly. On many aspects of this Bill, the Government have said in effect that details with regard to scrutiny, legislative competence and much of the working of the Bill in relation to the Assembly should be determined by the Assembly through its Standing Orders. It is believed that this is the right approach and that there is opposition to prescribing the arrangements for the composition of the Assembly's committees beyond that. The right approach to adopt is the very simple Scottish arrangement in which the composition of the committees is based on a very straightforward clause in the Scotland Act, which states that the composition and membership of the Parliament's committees should,

    "have due regard to the balance of parties within the Parliament".

Those are issues that exercise the presiding officer of the National Assembly.

19 Apr 2006 : Column 1142
 

The amendments would replace the title of the committee of the Assembly required by Clause 30. Clause 30 uses the term "Audit Committee", which my amendments would replace with "Accounts Committee". This might seem rather arcane. What is in a name? I argue that there is quite a lot in the name "audit committee", which is why it would be wise of the Government to ensure that there is no confusion about this committee. Noble Lords may expect me to start with audit committees in the private sector in particular, because that is where audit committees started life. But I shall start with audit committees as articulated by Her Majesty's Treasury in its 2003 audit committee handbook. The handbook deals with audit committees in central government, in executive agencies and NDPBs. The preamble to the handbook says:

    "The essence of the function of an audit committee in central government is to support the Accounting Officer (or Board) by monitoring and reviewing both the risk, control and governance processes which have been established in the organisation, and the associated assurance processes".

It refers to the,

    "independent perspective and a process of constructive challenge . . . . to help them"—

that is, the accounting officer or board—

    "to be fully assured that the most efficient, effective and economical risk, control and governance processes . . . are optimal".

So it is clear that the audit committees that are now commonplace throughout central government and, indeed, NDPBs are part of the processes within which the departments and other bodies manage their finances, internal controls and so on.

Things are a little different in the private sector. Listed companies look to the Combined Code on Corporate Governance, which sets out the basic requirement for them to have an audit committee. There are supporting guidelines specifically on audit committees, which are generally referred to as the Smith guidelines. The watch words in the Smith guidance are "oversight", "assessment" and "review" of the financial statements and the financial controls. The audit committee acts on behalf of the board in overseeing functions which are properly those of management. The core remit in the private sector is rather more narrowly drawn and focuses on financial statements and financial controls.

9.30 pm

With that background we come to the committee that is required by Clause 30, which is entitled "Audit Committee". To look at what that committee is expected to do we have to turn to Clause 142. The essence of the role of the committee is in subsection (1), which states:

    "The Audit Committee may consider, and lay before the Assembly a report on, any accounts, statement of accounts or report laid before the Assembly by . . . the Auditor General".

Noble Lords will see that that function is quite unlike functions of audit committees that exist either in the public or private sector. Indeed, its remit is pretty much that which is carried by the Public Accounts
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column 1143
 
Committee in another place. Indeed, by virtue of subsection (2) the audit committee may, if the Public Accounts Committee requests it, take evidence from Welsh accounting officers on behalf of the Public Accounts Committee. It emphasises that the committee required by Clause 30 is the Welsh equivalent of the arrangements that exist in another place, as enshrined in the Public Accounts Committee.

I believe that the language used by Clause 30 is, quite simply, confusing. It is clear that the work that the committee will be required to do is very like that of the Public Accounts Committee in another place, but it is quite unlike the kinds of audit committee that exist either in the public or private sector. It is not an advisory body or one that is supportive of an accounting officer as envisaged in the Treasury's handbook. Indeed, its work is to grill accounting officers on the basis of the Auditor General's reports. Accounting officers who have appeared before the Public Accounts Committee in another place have never described that process as supportive or advisory—and rightly so; nor is it an oversight body acting on behalf of a board, as envisaged in the private sector.

I understand that the Auditor General has made these general points to the Assembly committee which examined the Government's White Paper Better Governance for Wales and that the committee agreed. I hope that the Government will not saddle the new Welsh Government arrangements with an unnecessarily confusing title for a key committee. My solution is to call the committee an accounts committee to line up with the terminology that is used in another place.

What will be left after the embers have cooled is very uncertain. The position of the language under the Bill is obscure, to say the least. Under Section 47 of the 1998 Act, there was a duty laid on the Assembly as a corporate body, with executive and legislative functions, to give effect to the conduct of its business to the principle that the two languages should be treated on a basis of equality. The Assembly was also to have regard to the spirit of any guidelines under Section 9 of the 1993 Act, and Standing Orders were to be produced in both languages.

Now Section 47 is among the many from the 1998 Act that is to be repealed. As the noble Lord, Lord Prys-Davies, has pointed out, its ghost in emaciated form reappears in Clause 35, which imposes an obligation on the new Assembly in the conduct of Assembly proceedings to give effect to the principle of treating the two languages on the basis of equality. But no reference is made to guidelines under the 1993 Act or to Standing Orders being produced in both languages. Clause 31, which deals entirely with Standing Orders, states that the Clerk must publish them from time to time, but no reference is made to languages.

So there are many questions for the Government to answer about the differences between the old Section 47, which did provide protection for the language, and the lesser requirements and softer regime of new Clause 35, which links equality of language treatment with the totally different issue of equality of opportunity for all. Incidentally, equality of opportunity features among those special subjects like sustainable development that require Welsh Ministers to "pay special attention" to them. They have special clauses devoted to them, but no special clause is devoted to the protection of the Welsh language.

The main purpose of the noble Lord's new clause is to elevate the language, and especially Ministers' treatment of it, to what I understand is the special status accorded to the matters highlighted in the category of clauses grouped under the description,

Lord Evans of Temple Guiting: I start by paying tribute to the work that the noble Lord, Lord Roberts of Conwy, has done over the years in promoting the Welsh language.

Amendment No. 38 would insert a requirement for the Assembly's standing orders to be made in both English and Welsh. It is widely recognised that the Assembly has an excellent record of producing bilingual legislation. However, there have been moments when it has not been reasonably practicable to do so. For example, the Assembly's foot and mouth disease order describes several chemical tests which would not easily translate into Welsh. It would appear in that one case that the fact that it appears just in English is sufficient. Also, in 2005, the Assembly made a series of regulations regarding avian flu which were drafted in English only because of the severe time constraints imposed by decisions of the European Commission. It would have taken 20 days to translate, and then there was the possibility that urgent amendments might need to be made and those would have to be bilingual if the order was drafted in English and Welsh.

The Bill at present provides, in paragraph 18 of Schedule 11, that both the standing orders made by the Secretary of State, and the Assembly's proposals as to what provisions they should include, must be made in both English and Welsh. Subsequent revisions will be a matter for the Assembly. There is no reason to expect that these will not be made bilingually. The current Assembly's standing orders are in both Welsh and English. Any resolution of the Assembly to remake or revise the standing orders must be approved by at least two-thirds of the Assembly members voting. Any changes that were not proposed bilingually would be unlikely to secure sufficient support to be made. Furthermore, Clause 35 places obligations on the Assembly to treat English and Welsh on a basis of equality in the conduct of its proceedings. In drafting the Bill, the Government's approach has been to give the Assembly as much freedom as possible to make its own decisions about how it will work. This issue can properly be left to the Assembly to handle without the need for provision in the Bill.

Clause 1 [General duties of local authority in relation to well-being of young children]:

Baroness Morris of Bolton moved Amendment No. 1:

    Page 1, line 7, leave out "reduce inequalities between" and insert "show how they are raising the quality of outcomes of the most disadvantaged"

The noble Baroness said: In moving Amendment No. 1 and speaking to Amendment No. 4, I should like to say what a pleasure it is to be back in your Lordships' House and in Committee on the Childcare Bill. That would have removed the need for much of the additional material, but I am sure it will help to inform our debates throughout the Committee's proceedings and raise a few questions as well as answers.

The two amendments would remove the phrases "reduce inequalities between" and "reduction of inequalities between" in subsections 1(b) and 3(b) of Clause 1, which outlines the duty placed on local authorities. The amendments provide substitute wording, with emphasis placed on raising and improving the quality of outcomes for the most disadvantaged children. This key argument was raised by my honourable friends in another place; it goes to the heart of the thinking behind the Bill and raises fundamental questions about what reducing inequalities means, how it will be measured and how it will be delivered.
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC476
 

On Second Reading, Members on all sides of your Lordships' House agreed that we would like to enable all children to have the very best start in life, an aim that I wholeheartedly endorse. A good start in life will produce a better result for the child, their family and society as a whole and, as the Government point out, it will, one hopes, lessen the chances of needing later intervention. We believe that we must support and improve the well-being of children in the broadest sense but that it is vital to place the most vulnerable—the most disadvantaged—at the heart of our discussions and to focus resources to meet their needs.

Our concern is that the terminology in the Bill—"reduce inequalities"—lacks clarity and could lead to a levelling down rather than a pulling up to close the gap in provision and access to childcare services. The wording proposed in our amendments not only focuses on the quality of outcomes for the most disadvantaged but will require improvement in these outcomes and thus, we hope, a narrowing of the gap.

This issue stirred much spirited debate in the Commons. Out of curiosity, I took the time to look at the explanation in the Oxford English Dictionary of "reducing inequalities". "Reduce" is defined as "to bring down", or "to diminish", and "inequality" is defined as the,

    "want of equality between persons or things; disparity: in respect of magnitude, quantity, number, intensity, or other physical quality; a want of uniformity in a thing, persons or process; unevenness, irregularity, variableness".

While we want all children to have the best possible options, surely we do not want uniformity. Each child does not develop at a uniform pace in line with other children. We are concerned with each child's experience and opportunities, particularly those most in need. As I think we agreed at Second Reading, one size does not fit all.

In another place, the Minister disagreed with the terminology that we had chosen, yet in line 3 of paragraph 11 on page 3 of the government paper, Childcare Bill: Duty to improve well-being and reduce inequalities in outcomes for children up to 5—kindly provided by the Minister—the very first point states that the Bill "focuses on improving outcomes". I believe that my amendments go not only to the heart of this point, but one step further, in that we would specify on the face of the Bill the need for,

    "improvement in the quality of outcomes",

and not simply the number of outcomes.

Childcare should not become part of the tick-box mentality. More places do not necessarily mean better quality childcare and, thus, outcomes. Indeed, in the document that I just mentioned, paragraph 9 on page 2 says:

    "High quality early years provision has the potential to improve standards of health, education, economic potential and social interaction".

Yet the word "quality" is notably absent from the Bill.

Clauses 1 to 5 provide the statutory underpinning for integrated services, primarily delivered through Sure Start children's centres, with the aim that there should be a children's centre for every community
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC477
 
by 2010—a total of 3,500 nationwide. Can the Minister give us details of any modifications that have been made or are planned to be made to the Sure Start scheme? We are told that Sure Start centres are to play a central role in improving outcomes for all young children, so can the Minister say whether by that he means the quality of outcomes for the most disadvantaged children?

The test required by the words "reduce inequalities" could be met, grotesquely, with no improvement at all in the opportunity for the least advantaged if the opportunities for the more fortunate were reduced. That is not what we are about. Let us be plain in what we are about. We are about raising standards for all and, if we can, closing the gap between the least fortunate in their accidents of birth, culture and family prosperity. I hope that I can persuade the noble Baroness this afternoon that her concerns that the Bill could lead to levelling down, rather than levelling up, are entirely unfounded and that the points just made by the noble Baroness, Lady Walmsley, are entirely correct.

First, I believe that we all agree that children from less advantaged families do not, as a whole, do well enough at the moment and that, in a world of competing resources, it is right that new public resources should be particularly, although not exclusively, focused on those who are not doing sufficiently well and those families who are under pressure. The noble Baroness accepted that in her opening remarks.

Secondly, when it comes to childcare and early childhood experiences, privately funded family provision is of course the dominant element of provision, and rightly so, for better-off families. That provision meets many if not most of the needs of the better-off, without a requirement for public support or intervention on anything like the scale that is needed to support those who are less well off, especially those who are struggling to juggle work and childcare or the needs of disabled children in terms of finance and the availability of provision, which are the areas of special emphasis of the opening clauses of the Bill. Hence the provisions in Clauses 1 to 4, which give local authorities both a general duty to improve the well-being of young children in their area—it is essential to stress that first duty, which responds to the noble Baroness's concern that there could be a levelling down—and a duty to use their powers to reduce inequalities between young children in their area; that is, to prioritise their intervention clearly and strongly on those who are least well off by demonstrating that they are narrowing the gap between the bottom and the rest.

As the noble Baroness also wants us to raise the floor, if I may put it that way, and her amendment specifically calls for an improvement in,

    "the quality of outcomes of the most disadvantaged",

it could only—this is my third point—water down that objective if the improvement is not to be relative as well as absolute. We want both. I was initially surprised that the noble Baroness should want to water down the Bill's proposals but, as she said, we both read the debates in the other place, where there was a good deal of what is best described as political knock-about on levelling up and levelling down and why it would theoretically be possible—of course we accept that—to narrow a gap by having a less rapid level of improvement among the most advantaged compared to the least advantaged.
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC479
 

Not only would such levelling down be explicitly contrary to Clause 1(1)(a), under which a local authority in England must improve the well-being of young children in its area, but it is mind-boggling to try to understand how a local authority could go about reducing the advantages and performance of the more advantaged. I do not know what the hallucinations were of colleagues of the noble Baroness in another place concerning what a dreadful Labour government might do. What was she thinking that we might do to hold back the development of young children from better-off families? Is it contended that we might stop them having the great advantages of synthetic phonics when we teach them to read? I could go through a list of things that I might imagine a government might do, but I find it almost inconceivable that any government would do them, even a government that did not have the interests of the community at large at heart in the way that this Government have.

The contention that a local authority might seek to pursue policies that did not improve the well-being of young people in general in their area would be hard to sustain. It is almost impossible to posit a set of circumstances in which a local authority could bring that about.

4 pm

In any event—this is my fourth point—the noble Baroness does not need to look into a crystal ball. She can simply look at the abundant evidence of what the Government have done in this area over the last eight years. It is clear that, far from levelling down, there has in fact been a significant extension of under-five services to everyone, including substantial improvements in nursery provision on a universal basis with no means-testing whatever. I believe our bona fides as levellers-up, not levellers-down, are unimpeachable, and there is no need for concern about our intentions in that regard.

I shall give a few facts to sustain that point. In 1997, publicly funded nursery provision for four year-olds was patchy, and there was very little for three year-olds. Now, there is 12 and a half hours' provision for 33 weeks for all parents of three and four year-olds who wish to take it up, as the overwhelming majority now do. From 1 April, that provision has been extended to 38 weeks as a universal entitlement, funded by an additional £82 million a year which my department is providing in the next two years of the spending review within the dedicated schools grant to support the extended hours. In the 10-year childcare strategy, we have committed to extending not only the number of weeks that the universal minimum free early education entitlement is available, but also the number of hours per week, from 12 and a half to 15 hours per week for 38 weeks of the year.

We also set out our longer-term aspiration, when we go into the by then third decade of this Labour Government, that there should be 20 hours a week for 28 weeks for every child. That needs to be set in the context not only of the significant expansion we have seen of under-five and nursery provision, but also of
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC480
 
the big increase in child benefit paid universally to all parents; the increase in paid maternity leave, extended from six to nine months from April 2007, again available to all mothers; the introduction for the first time in this country of paid paternity leave; and the childcare element of the working tax credit, which is paid up to a family income level of £59,000 a year, well up into the middle and higher reaches of the income scale, and which is now received by well over 300,000 families at a public cost of over £800 million a year. So I do not think that any fear that we are levelling down is well founded. You simply need to look at what we are doing in terms of the universal provision, alongside the extra targeted provision for those who are less well off, to see that our bona fides in this regard are entirely clear and above suspicion.

My last words on these amendments are taken from a contribution from Maria Miller, one of the noble Baroness's colleagues in another place, who put the argument very well when the House of Commons debated the Childcare Bill on Report last month:

    "In Committee, some philosophical differences were expressed about the reduction of inequalities versus improvement in the quality of outcomes . . . but we may be closer together than the Committee debate suggested. I refer to an interview given by the Prime Minister on 'Newsnight' in 2001, when he clearly stated that the Government's objective was to level things up. He said: 'Surely the important thing is to level up those people that don't have opportunity in our society'. We wholeheartedly endorse that view . . . Our philosophy is simple: to maximise the potential of children so that no child is held back or left behind".—[Official Report, Commons, 9/3/06; col. 1005.]

I entirely endorse those sentiments. They are precisely the reason why we have set out these duties on local authorities in the way that we have, so that local authorities should have two interlocking duties: to improve the well-being of young people in their area—that is, to level up for all children in their area—while at the same time seeking, as an objective on which to focus the public resources that they have available, to reduce inequalities between young children in their area with regard to the matters mentioned in subsection (2).

Let me make a final point on Sure Start. We have sought to improve the practice guidance and the delivery of Sure Start programmes to meet some of the issues raised in the Birkbeck study, to which the noble Baroness referred. But, of course, the big change which will be ongoing in Sure Start as we move ahead in the next five years is the change from Sure Start children's centres being focused exclusively on poorer areas to being universally available nationwide. I do not think that there could be a clearer demonstration of our commitment to levelling up and to making services available for all and not simply focusing, as we should, on the most disadvantaged and those parents who face the most difficulty when their children are at the most vulnerable point in their life.

I hope that I have said enough to persuade the noble Baroness that we are probably not as far apart as she may have thought.

It is a privilege to be working on this Bill. This group of amendments has two main themes, as the noble Baroness, Lady Morris, said. As my noble friend explained earlier, Clauses 1 to 5 should be read together. It may help if I explain the context of the provisions in Clauses 1 to 5 and how the development of early childhood services fits within the children's trusts arrangements. Many Members of the Committee worked on the Children Act 2004. I did not have the privilege of following this brief then, but many of you will know that Section 10 of the Children Act 2004 creates duties on local authorities and the relevant partners listed in that Act, to make arrangements to promote co-operation between them with a view to improving the well-being of all children and young people in their area. It is under those provisions that the children's trust arrangements have been implemented and children and young people's plans drawn up.

The greatest chance of success in fulfilling the outcomes duties will come where those responsible for early childhood services work closely together, within the overall umbrella of the children's trust arrangements. We want the duties under the Bill to be a central part of children's trusts, as partnership working provides the means for progress in early childhood services for our youngest children. So, while we have not simply copied that 2004 Act, it is important that the new provisions should be compatible with it. I should also stress that the Government fully recognise that parents are a hugely
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC488
 
important factor in determining whether outcomes are improved for children. We are committed to ensuring that parents are actively involved in the delivery of the duties, and the Bill already fully reflects the importance of parents.

There are explicit requirements in the Bill for local authorities to involve parents in carrying out their duties to improve outcomes and reduce inequalities, to ensure that early childhood services reach out to excluded families, and to provide information and advice to parents about childcare and other services for children and young people.

Let me take Amendment No. 2 first. "Well-being" is defined in both Clause 1 of this Bill and Section 10 of the 2004 Act. This is one instance where we have quite deliberately copied the existing definition—and for good reasons, as I will explain—but this amendment would add a sixth category to that definition. As drafted, Clause 1(2) defines "well-being" in terms of the five Every Child Matters outcomes; it is worth describing them again now. Being healthy relates to physical, mental and emotional well-being; staying safe means protecting from harm or neglect; and enjoying and achieving refers to play, education and training. The other two are making a positive contribution and achieving social and economic well-being. As the Committee will appreciate, those five ECM outcomes were drawn up to include the matters that children and young people themselves said were most important to them. They are designed to encompass every aspect of a child's life and were endorsed in the 2004 Act.

I agree that the quality of a child's relationship with its parents or carers is vital in promoting a sense of security and cultivating an environment in which that child will thrive and develop. That must particularly be the case with babies and the youngest children who are in view in Clause 1. That is why we have specifically included such services as family support and parenting services in the definition of early childhood services in Clause 2, because we recognise the importance of helping families to establish strong, supportive relationships. Surely, that is the key. The relationship between children and their carers is a process, which will continue throughout that child's life, one hopes. It is one that we want to support and strengthen through the Bill. However, I see it as a process rather than an outcome. The credo of the five outcomes is the result of maximising support for parents and carers.

As I explained earlier, it is these services which, under Clause 3, local authorities and their partners must plan and deliver in integrated ways that maximise access and the benefits to the children and parents using them. However, there must be limits in how far we go in making local authorities directly responsible for the quality of relationships within families, which is what we would be doing as part of a duty if we added this sixth phrase to the Every Child Matters outcomes. Of course, children greatly value a happy home life, but it is not necessary to add this sixth category to the definition of well-being; the effect is already achieved by the existing five outcomes.
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC489
 

Several of the outcomes could impact on whether family relationships are put under stress. For example, being protected from harm or neglect is key, and, as the noble Baroness, Lady Walmsley, said, it is hard to envisage how it would be possible to achieve emotional well-being if a young child were not growing up within a healthy, supportive, relationship that meant that they felt secure in the knowledge that they were loved and cared for.

The amendment would also have implications for Clause 39. That clause sets out the requirements of the early years foundation stage, which must be delivered by early years providers—that is, providers of integrated learning and childcare. The stated purpose of the EYFS in Clause 39 is to promote the well-being of young children attending early years settings, but it will, in any case, address the way in which young children build relationships, without the need for this amendment. We will discuss the early years foundation stage when we reach it later on in the Bill, but I can assure the Committee that the importance of every child developing a strong sense of self and secure attachment is central to our approach. A child's secure emotional development through positive attachment is intrinsic to the "personal, social and emotional development" area of learning, which is already part of this Bill. The EYFS will require providers to work constructively with parents to meet the individual needs of each child and, by delivering the EYFS, providers will support children in forming good relationships with other children and adults.

The five Every Child Matters outcomes are fundamental to the 10-year strategy and are now very familiar to professionals and parents alike. We see them on the walls of children's centres, schools and nurseries all over the country. Amendment No. 2 would create a mismatch with how those outcomes are set for the purpose of Every Child Matters and, most important, with the legal definition in the 2004 Act, which would have far-reaching implications for other provisions. For example, the Act defines the purpose of both children's trust arrangements and the children and young people's plan. For those reasons, I hope that I have persuaded the Committee that Amendment No. 2 is unnecessary, and that the noble Baronesses will feel able to withdraw it.

Amendment No. 10 proposes replicating words from the Children Act 2004 in Clause 4 of the Bill. We fully acknowledge the importance of maintaining consistency with the 2004 Act. We are building on the partnership arrangements in that Act, under which children's trust arrangements were established, and are developing the approach in ways that best suit the needs of young children. This amendment would add a new subsection to Clause 4, replicating Section 10(3) of the Children Act 2004, which requires children's services authorities to have regard to the importance of parents and other persons caring for children in improving the well-being of young children. I assure noble Lords that the definition of "parents" is widely drafted and incorporates the biological mothers and
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC490
 
fathers of a child, including non-residential fathers and mothers, as well as others with formal parental responsibility. It also includes any individual who has care of the child, such as the local authority as the corporate parent of looked-after children.

I have already stressed the huge significance that the Government place on the role that both parents and other persons caring for children play in improving the well-being of young children. The role of parents and others caring for children is already an inherent part of Clauses 1 to 5 of the Bill. Clause 3 specifically states that local authorities,

    "must take all reasonable steps to encourage and facilitate the involvement of parents and prospective parents"

in the planning and delivery of integrated early childhood services designed to improve the well-being of young children and to reduce inequalities. Under Clause 3, local authorities have a specific duty to ensure that there is an outreach service that identifies parents who are unlikely to take advantage of early childhood services. That is very important. One of the lessons from the early days of Sure Start is that we need to get to the people who actually need assistance. The outreach service will encourage parents to take advantage of these services. This recognises the importance of proactively helping disadvantaged and excluded families to benefit from early childhood services.

Finally, a key component of integrated early childhood services, as outlined in Clause 2, is the local authority duty under Clause 12 to provide information and assistance to parents about childcare and other services in their area and facilities or publications that may be of benefit to children or their parents.

I hope that I have demonstrated with this rather lengthy reply that this set of clauses already takes full account of the vital role that parents and other people caring for children play in improving outcomes and narrowing inequalities. While fully endorsing the 2004 Act, we believe that we can build on that approach and therefore go further than Amendment No. 10 proposes in creating positive duties on local authorities for the benefit of parents and young children. I therefore see no added value in accepting Amendment No. 10.

19 Apr 2006 : Column GC495
 

These duties build on the practice developed by the Sure Start programme, which stimulated the growth of integrated early childhood services in disadvantaged areas through dedicated ring-fenced funding. By bringing this Bill before Parliament, we are making clear our commitment to develop early childhood services as a core part of mainstream public services in the long term, creating a wholly new area of the welfare state.

However, the fact remains that local authorities' involvement in Sure Start is a relatively recent innovation, contrasting with more than a century of responsibility for public education. Early years services are still finding their place in local authorities' priorities. The new duties to improve outcomes aim to underpin early childhood services and give them equivalent status to more established services. It is to ensure that these duties are taken sufficiently seriously and, let me stress, given equivalent importance in the eyes of local authorities to their well-established education and schools functions that we believe that statutory targets have a role to play—I stress "a" role to play—in complementing all the other processes that have been discussed.

Concern has been expressed that we are trying to create a target culture with unintended effects. I hope that the Members of the Committee who have had a chance to read in the document that I sent before Easter, to which the noble Baroness, Lady Morris, referred, the draft outline of the statutory guidance on the duty to improve well-being and reduce inequalities in outcomes for children up to five will have been reassured. In paragraphs 20 and 21, a number of important statements are made which I believe meet a number of the concerns raised by both noble Baronesses.

First, as the noble Baroness, Lady Morris, rightly said, the targets will be developed in conjunction with local authorities. Paragraph 20 is explicit that there should be a proper process of consultation with local authorities and says that,

    "if the Secretary of State proposes to set targets which differ from any proposed by the local authority, she must give an account of her reasons and allow at least one month for the authority to make further representations".

So there is a very strong emphasis on collaborative partnership in the development of targets and a requirement for specific reasons to be given by the Secretary of State if the department wishes there to be targets that are different from those which the local authority propose.

It is also set out clearly in paragraph 21 that the target setting will take place following existing priorities meetings—to be held in the autumn—between strategic advisers, government offices, the local authority, health services and other key partners invited by the authority. That is an established part of the relationship between local authorities, the inspection agencies and the departments. Such meetings are, for example, the forums in which discussions take place between local authorities and national strategies advisers of my department for their work in the schools area, which leads in due course to
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC496
 
the establishment of targets there, too. So this follows a recognised process and is not, we believe, an undue imposition.

The other key element in this is that the targets that we are setting are not arbitrary and related in some way to areas that would, as the noble Baroness, Lady Walmsley, suggested, detract from child development. They are specifically related, as paragraph 20 of the draft guidance states, to children's development as measured in the foundation stage profile, which I hope Members of the Committee have had a chance to see. If not, I shall be happy to circulate it.

The foundation stage profile is a very serious and comprehensive assessment tool. It has been welcomed by all the early years professionals for the extremely comprehensive way that it develops approaches to the measurement of child development. The profile was developed in conjunction with a large range of early years practitioners, with a number of leading university education departments that specialise in these areas, with a foundation stage working group, which had a whole host of expert members attached to it, with a practitioner panel and with a special educational needs panel. It has been very widely supported and it has the 13 areas of early years development which are in the foundation stage profile itself. If Members of the Committee look at it, I believe that they will find it thoroughly comprehensive.

The profile covers dispositions and attitudes, social development, emotional development, language for communication and thinking, linking sounds and letters, reading, writing, numbers as labels and for counting—so even the concern of the noble Earl, Lord Listowel, about mathematical development is covered—calculating shapes, space and measures, and knowledge and understanding of the world. That last item is not, as I thought when I first looked at it, a test of whether four year-olds know about the European Union and other elements of the curriculum; it is about showing curiosity and interest by exploring surroundings and observing, selecting and manipulating objects and materials, which sounds thoroughly appropriate. The profile also covers physical development, which is absolutely crucial, and creative development, which is equally important.

The aggregate results of the assessment by the early years professionals of the development of children under these 13 headings, each of which has nine sub-headings which the professionals used comprehensively to assess the development of children, are the basis for the statistics. Those statistics are then collected by local authorities—they are collected now; the process is taking place at the moment—to assess the development of under-fives in their area. They are then aggregated by the department. Indeed, every year we publish the results of the foundation stage profile, which include highlighting the comparative levels of development of children in each of these 13 areas. As we say clearly in the draft guidance that we have circulated, the foundation stage profile carries the confidence of the

5.15 pm

I did not sufficiently reply to one of the points made by the noble Baroness, Lady Morris, which is what happens if the targets that are not met and if the
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC499
 
inspection process leads to significant findings of failure. We are committed to proportionate responses and we made it very clear in the draft guidance that any intervention by central government would be proportionate, as would obviously be expected. However, I do not hesitate to say to the Committee that, as it would expect of a government, where there is evidence of significant failure, we have a duty on behalf of the public to ensure that provision is made locally for services that are not being properly conducted and managed to be properly conducted and managed. Where there is evidence of failure—for example, identified by Ofsted—we would expect to hold early discussions with local authorities and, wherever possible, agree solutions tailored to the circumstances of the authority, with the intention of avoiding more draconian action. However, the Secretary of State has more substantial powers of intervention, all of which will be used only given a substantial evidence base that services are failing locally.

To give an example of a recent intervention, in Swindon in 2001, in response to a very critical Ofsted inspection, the Secretary of State issued a direction to outsource the strategic management of Swindon's education department. Tribal Group plc was awarded a three-year contract up to August last year. To support corporate improvement, an education partnership board was put in place to provide impartial advice and guidance to the council. That provided a significant level of direction, but also support for the authority from professionals who were able to meet its capacity weaknesses. Later inspections reported highly satisfactory progress in education following that intervention and the inspectors commented in particular on the positive effect of the strategic partnership between the education partnership board, the work of Tribal and the local authority. As a result, the direction was lifted at the end of August last year.

I have a number of other examples where interventions have taken place. Such interventions have a long established history in local education authorities. I should say that the power to intervene does not always mean outsourcing; it means taking appropriate action, which can often mean agreeing with the local authority significant changes in its corporate management or processes to ensure that defined and serious weaknesses are met, leading to real change on the ground.

In conclusion, effective delivery and planning of services must depend on close and effective partnership between local and central government. It requires both to fulfil their roles. As part of that process, central government has a role in acting as the ultimate guardian of the public interest. Where local authorities are, in the judgment of professionals and inspecting bodies, not delivering services effectively, we have a duty to intervene to bring about change. However, that is an exceptional situation and, as we
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC500
 
have made clear in the guidance, all interventions under the Bill will be proportionate and related only to clear and defined evidence of weakness and failure.

reported is a very substantial document; it sets out, in a good deal of detail, what the progress has been in the areas covered by the profile.

Clause 1 places a duty on local authorities to improve the well-being of young children and to reduce inequalities between them by delivering integrated early childhood services and by acting in a way that should achieve these two objectives. We absolutely agree that monitoring and evaluation of these duties is important. We do not wish to create new and burdensome arrangements for local authorities; instead, we are committed to making the most effective use of existing inspection mechanisms and the existing and steadily refined early years profile. That is why we have made sure that there are provisions in the Bill to bring all the new duties on local authorities in Part 1 within existing processes for doing this.

Clauses 14 and 16 already cover the inspection and accountability of the duties in Part 1. Clause 16 places responsibilities for the duties with the director of children's services and the lead member for children's services, as established by the Children Act 2004. Adding these new duties to the definition of children's services in the Act also has the effect of bringing them within the remit of joint area reviews mentioned by the noble Baroness. Clause 14 brings the new duties within Ofsted's inspection remit, and Clause 15 allows the Secretary of State to intervene if there is evidence of poor performance, as we discussed in the debate on the previous amendment.

Ofsted, with other inspectorates, assesses the council's arrangements in each case for children's services through annual performance assessments and the wider provision by the council and other agencies through joint area reviews. Annual performance assessment scores and joint area review reports must be sent to the Secretary of State, and that requirement will continue under the new regime.

Key inspection documents are already made public. Annual performance assessment scores and letters setting out strengths and weaknesses are placed on Ofsted's website, as are joint area review reports. As I have said, the results of the annual foundation stage profile are also published. A local authority receiving a joint area review report must also publish the report locally, in a manner prescribed by the Children Act 2004 (Joint Area Reviews) Regulations 2005.

In addition, with the exception of authorities deemed to be "excellent", all local authorities are required to prepare and publish their children and young people's plan, setting out the authority's strategy for improving children's well-being. During its preparation, they are required to consult children, young people and their families. Each plan should be published on or before 1 April 2006; it will be placed on the authority's website and will be made available in hard copy in public libraries and other public places. The authority is required to review and renew the plan each year.

In view of those existing arrangements, we think that it would be neither necessary nor appropriate to require authorities to make a separate report of their
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC505
 
contribution to young children, as set out in the amendment. Further reporting would only add unnecessary bureaucracy and divert resources away from young children.

The second issue that the noble Baroness addresses is the voice of the child. Her amendment is intended to include the views of young children in performance evaluation. Of course, we completely support her desire to ensure that there is a full and proper reflection of the voice of children in the development of services and we agree that the views of children under five, as well as those over five, are valuable. However, we do not believe that this is the right place for the voice of the child to be heard under statutory changes.

We agree that it is important for young children to be active in the day-to-day matters of the settings that they attend and that their views should be listened to when decisions are made. The noble Baroness, Lady Howarth, and I have been in correspondence about that recently and she sent me some powerful research work that demonstrates the cogency and coherence with which young children can, from a very early age, express their views and the importance of ensuring that those views are taken into account in the development of services. I am entirely persuaded, as is my department, by the findings of that research.

However, the question is whether in the development of strategic provision, which is what this part of the Bill concerns, this is an appropriate place in which to impose new statutory duties. Although much good work can take place at the level of settings and individual providers, there are obvious practical difficulties in engaging this age group in meaningful consultation about the strategic issues of the location and planning of provision across local authority areas. For example, whether an authority has been effective in reducing inequalities or whether a children's centre is in the best place are not questions on which it would be easy or, probably, possible, to consult under-fives themselves. Trying to convey the concept of well-being or inequality is simply not appropriate for children of that age.

Effectively engaging children in the development of the services that they use is a specialist area, requiring a careful and skilled approach to delivery on the ground. That is why, in the children's centre practice guidance issued last September, we encourage local authorities to adopt approaches to engage children that have proved effective elsewhere—for example, using the Thomas Coram Listening to Young Children resource.

Our practice guidance also gives details of ways to explore what young children really think. For example, through the use of painting, cameras, music or storytelling—not simply in what the noble Baroness rightly says is an inappropriate way through web consultations—it is possible to establish what very young children like or dislike. I am sure that we will see further tools being produced as practice is further developed. Where appropriate, there will be references in future guidance to those tools.
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC506
 

I can also assure the noble Baroness that this will be a key feature of the early years foundation stage, which will emphasise the importance of professionals listening to all young children in their care, in line with what is currently a routine approach to early learning. Indeed, we have already rolled out nationally training on the Thomas Coram Listening to Young Children: a participatory approach materials, specifically to encourage early years practitioners to seek and act on children's views. In developing the early years foundation stage, we have also through four local authorities actively consulted parents and children.

The Bill requires local authorities actively to encourage and facilitate the involvement of parents and prospective parents when planning early childhood services. For the youngest children and babies, parents are likely to have the best sense of what their children think and feel about the services that they use. When we consider local authorities' plans for children's centres, we discuss with the authorities the effectiveness of their mechanisms for consulting parents. Also, as part of the new performance management system that we are introducing for local authorities from April 2007, we will include a measure about parental satisfaction with the services that authorities are delivering. Parents will instinctively include their children's views in their responses to surveys.

I therefore believe that we have anticipated the issues on which the amendment touches and have made appropriate provision for them. Our concern is to avoid creating burdensome and inflexible new processes over and above those; we would prefer to leave local authorities to deliver services that meet local needs building on existing best practice. For all those reasons, we hope that the noble Baroness will be satisfied with our approach.

The noble Baroness said: I will speak also to Amendment No. 46, which is grouped with it. one of the outcomes under the Children Act 2004.

The Government have been supportive of listening to young children, and we heard a great deal more about that today from the Minister. We all welcome that commitment. They have committed, through funding for research and responding to urgings from this House, to put the voice of the child into other pieces of legislation. However, the Government must be consistent and put it in here too. It is important enough to need to be in the statute, not just in the guidance. The Childcare Bill is a real opportunity to declare in statute that listening to the voice of the child includes very young children just as much as older children and young people. There are so many advantages in doing so, not just for the quality and accessibility of services, but for individual children too, when they make their contribution. Children who feel their views are valued are able to develop their self esteem, independence and ability to make choices.

Involving children in decision-making can present challenges. That is why the phrase "all reasonable steps" is appropriate for this age range. Although those working in local authorities may not have the skills to consult young children, a well-trained children's workforce practitioner can easily do so and feed those views into the decision-making process. Of course, we are not talking about toddlers attending committee meetings or contributing to focus groups. Mind you, we might get some common sense out of them.

Although I welcome indications that statutory guidance under the Bill will,
 
19 Apr 2006 : Column GC511
 

    "expect that the views of young children also be actively sought and taken into account",

I am concerned that this may be limited to children's centres. The need to listen to children is a whole sector need, and should apply to every kind of setting.

I have submitted amendments, not just to the arrangements about the type of services provided, in Clause 11, but also to Clause 3, about where the services should be. That has been the subject of the most vehement rejection by the Minister in conversations that we have had. I see no reason why young children should not be able to express views about how far they want to travel to reach their services, or whether they should have to pass through crime-ridden areas or estates to get there that would make them feel threatened. Those are matters where it is perfectly appropriate to get a child's perspective. Where an adult would not feel threatened, a child might. Where an adult may not mind a three-bus journey, with no room for a push chair, a child might. Amending Clause 3 to include the voice of the child is just as appropriate as amending Clause 11.

I conclude by giving a short case history, which shows that we cannot always predict what children will think. In a setting in Brent, a practitioner asked each child what they would like to change about the nursery. One child answered that she would like to change her mind. She was not being cute or sharp. When the practitioner looked into it and watched that child at play, she found that the nursery was so laid out that the child could not easily change her activity to play with something else without the help of an adult. In other words, the ability of the child to make choices and change her mind was being limited by the actual layout of the nursery. You will not be surprised to learn that something was done about it. Things look different when you are less than a metre tall and not very strong. That is why I beg to move.

We argue that the inclusion of the terminology "promote co-operation between" in the context of this clause is vital. There will have to be a holistic, joined-up approach for the ambitious provisions envisaged by the Bill to work, and that will require joint responsibility between the local authority and its partners. Co-operation will emphasise the obligation on other parties—the police, health authorities and so on—to play an integral part of the promotion of the well-being and the welfare of young children.

It was argued by the Minister in the other place that the use of "co-operation" was weaker and less specific than the use of the word "work", which was more proactive. I disagree. I refer once more to the Oxford English Dictionary, which was well thumbed over the Easter Recess. It defines co-operation as,

    "working together towards the same end, purpose, or effect; a joint operation".

This, to me, installs exactly what the Minister wants—proactiveness.
